<span id="freeTextContainer3535288609912601340">Horatia Winwood, a stammering girl barely out of the schoolroom, takes the place of her beautiful older sister (who is romantically involved elsewhere) in entering into a marriage of convenience with the wealthy Earl of Rule. The tentative understanding between the new husband and wife is complicated when Rule's old enemy, Lord Lethbridge, takes an interest in his young br</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3535288609912601340">Horatia Winwood, a stammering girl barely out of the schoolroom, takes the place of her beautiful older sister (who is romantically involved elsewhere) in entering into a marriage of convenience with the wealthy Earl of Rule. The tentative understanding between the new husband and wife is complicated when Rule's old enemy, Lord Lethbridge, takes an interest in his young bride, in revenge over a scandal of several years earlier.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_14622167">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14622167" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/56479-res" class="left imgcol" title="Res"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1177127447p2/56479.jpg" alt="Res" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14622167?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 07, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/56479-res" class="user" title="Res">Res</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/32106.The_Convenient_Marriage" title="Paperback 9780099474425" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/56479-res?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14622167">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3109096784735536072">The one where the notorious rake Lord Rule decides to marry the pretty Winwood sister, but the reckless one, Horatia, persuades him to marry her instead. She gambles; her brother Pelham gambles (in fact, his gambling is the reason the Winwood sisters are prepared to marry Rule to begin with); hijinks ensue.<br /><br />Horatia is kind of an idiot, isn't she? I mean, a little boldness is refreshing in a Regency heroine, but Horatia seems like an eighth-grader who can be induced to do anything by being told th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3109096784735536072">The one where the notorious rake Lord Rule decides to marry the pretty Winwood sister, but the reckless one, Horatia, persuades him to marry her instead. She gambles; her brother Pelham gambles (in fact, his gambling is the reason the Winwood sisters are prepared to marry Rule to begin with); hijinks ensue.<br /><br />Horatia is kind of an idiot, isn't she? I mean, a little boldness is refreshing in a Regency heroine, but Horatia seems like an eighth-grader who can be induced to do anything by being told that Mom doesn't want her to do it. It's a little creepy to think of someone so immature being married at all.<br /><br />I did like Rule; he reminded me of <a title=" Diana Wynne Jones" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Diana%20Wynne%20Jones"> Diana Wynne Jones</a>'s Chrestomanci, with his vagueness and his deceptive intelligence and the laziness that makes everyone underestimate him. (At one point, his sister tells him he should beat Horatia, and he says, "But think how fatiguing!")<br /><br />The scene where drunken Pel and his drunken friend Pom rescue Horatia and try to figure out why she's out in the streets alone is hilarious, but by the end of the book I'd had a little too much Pel and Pom getting into scrapes. <br /><br />And -- did noblemen really suck on the heads of their canes? She mentions this <em>twice,</em> and it sounds unhygienic, not to mention really strange-looking. <br /><br />Oh, and if you're looking to buy a copy, don't get the paperback edition with the foreword by Jo Beverley; it's <em>full</em> of typos.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3109096784735536072">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_113677291">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113677291" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1406509-laurel" class="left imgcol" title="Laurel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442531711p2/1406509.jpg" alt="Laurel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/113677291?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 09, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1406509-laurel" class="user" title="Laurel">Laurel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7958128-the-convenient-marriage" title="Audio CD 9781843794417" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1406509-laurel?shelf=regency-era-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">regency-era-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1406509-laurel?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1406509-laurel?shelf=geogette-heyer" class="actionLinkLite">geogette-heyer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer113677291">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer375464733071729481"><strong>Richard Armitage reading a Georgette Heyer classic! *swoon*</strong><br /><br />I had not read <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> before this new Naxos Audiobooks recording happily landed on my doorstep. I will confess all up front. I did the unthinkable. I read the complete plot synopsis on Wikipedia before I delved into the first chapter. *horrors* Don’t even think about following my example. It will spoil the most enjoyable aspect of this novel – surprise!<br /><br /><em>The Convenient Marriage</em> is one of Georgette Heyer’s more popular Geor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText375464733071729481"><strong>Richard Armitage reading a Georgette Heyer classic! *swoon*</strong><br /><br />I had not read <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> before this new Naxos Audiobooks recording happily landed on my doorstep. I will confess all up front. I did the unthinkable. I read the complete plot synopsis on Wikipedia before I delved into the first chapter. *horrors* Don’t even think about following my example. It will spoil the most enjoyable aspect of this novel – surprise!<br /><br /><em>The Convenient Marriage</em> is one of Georgette Heyer’s more popular Georgian-era rom-com’s, and for good reason. It has all the requisite winning elements: a wealthy and eligible hero, a young naïve heroine, greedy relatives, a scheming mistress and a revengeful rake. Add in a duel, a sword fight, highway robbery, abduction, switched identities and scandalous behavior, and you are in for comedic high jinxes and uproarious plot twists. As I laughed out loud at the preposterous plot machinations in the synopsis, I thought to myself, <em>“How does Heyer do it? How can she take us on such an outrageously wild ride and make it believable?”</em> I was soon to find out.<br /><br />Handsome and elegant Marcus Drelincourt, Earl of Rule, is comfortable in his bachelorhood. At thirty-five his sister Lady Louisa Quain urges him to marry, suggesting the beautiful Elizabeth Winwood. She is from an aristocratic family of good pedigree but little fortune. With two unmarried younger sisters, prim Charlotte and impulsive Horatia, and their self-indulgent elder brother Pelham (about as much help to his family as a rainstorm at a picnic), she must marry well. Lady Winwood is thrilled when the Earl agrees to marry Elizabeth and save the family from destitution. Seventeen-year old Horatia is not. Presenting herself at the Earl’s doorstep she boldly offers herself to him in exchange for her elder sister who is in love with Lieutenant Edward Heron. Horry proposes a marriage of convenience to Lord Rule with the promise that she will not interfere with him after they are married. She does not bring much to the bargain. Not only is she poor, she does not possess her sister’s beauty, and she stutters. Intrigued by this young, brave girl, he is tempted and soon sees the logic, agreeing to her proposal.<br /><br />The new Countess of Rule wastes no time in becoming the sensation of the bon ton dressing to the nines, attending parties, the opera, gambling huge sums and getting into all sorts of scrapes while her husband continues to pay attentions to his mistress Lady Caroline Massey. With patience and fortitude, Lord Rule councils his stubborn young bride against excess and the dangerous liaisons of Baron Robert Lethbridge, a known rake with a history with the Drelincourt family.<br /><br />Determined to teach her husband a lesson for his interference, she defies his wishes attending a masked ball. Escorted by Lethbridge, he sees their friendship as the perfect opportunity to ruin her reputation and punish Drelincourt for thwarting his elopement with his sister Louisa years before. Horry tempts Lethbridge with cards, bending his resistance by scandalously agreeing to offer a lock of her hair if he wins. Unbeknownst to Horry, her husband has followed her to the ball, overhears their conversation and intercedes by stepping on her dress and ripping it. While she is away he disposes of Lethbridge and exchanges his costume with his own. Returning, Horry loses badly at cards and must give Lethbridge/Rule his winnings. Penitent, she concedes the bet which is met with a stolen kiss. Furious, Horry rushes away running into Lady Caroline Massey who recognizes her. Certain that her husband’s mistress will reveal to him that his wife was at the ball, she confesses all to him first. The Earl in turn reveals his charade. Discovering that he has fallen in love with his wife, how will he court and convince her that love is much better than a marriage of convenience?<br /><br />Heyer’s characterizations just sparkle and shine. This May/Decemeber relationship presents great opportunity for difference in opinion and blunder. If Horry had not been an impulsive, stubborn seventeen-year old there would have been little conflict and no story. Lord Rule’s patience in dealing with his teenage bride commanded respect, endearing us to him by opening up the possibility of the love relationship that we hope for. This delightful romp was made all the more enjoyable by this new audio recording by British stage and screen actor Richard Armitage. This is his third foray into Georgette Heyer for Naxos Audiobooks. His skill at unique characterization and resonant, velvetly voice transports the listener like Cinderella to the Ball. Unfortunately, once the story ends, so does the enchantment. My solution was to start it again. For me, a new audio recording combining fanciful storyteller Georgette Heyer and the sultry and seductive voice of Richard Armitage is like la petite mort. Hopefully they are not few and far between.<br /><br />Laurel Ann, Austenprose.com</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="375464733071729481">...more</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1023270571">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11540503897837910918">One of those impetuous, immature heroines I dislike so much, with a stammer that is written out Every.Single.Time; lots of frantic, farcical running around; an evil mistress and a dastardly villain; and a hero who's twice the age of the heroine. Also, romances where one of the main characters is committing adultery, <em>regardless</em> of the circumstances (here it's that they've agreed in advance to a marriage of pure convenience), always have a huge strike against them in my book. To be fair, this stor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11540503897837910918">One of those impetuous, immature heroines I dislike so much, with a stammer that is written out Every.Single.Time; lots of frantic, farcical running around; an evil mistress and a dastardly villain; and a hero who's twice the age of the heroine. Also, romances where one of the main characters is committing adultery, <em>regardless</em> of the circumstances (here it's that they've agreed in advance to a marriage of pure convenience), always have a huge strike against them in my book. To be fair, this story is more of a comedy than a romance, but still. Anyway, it all adds up to way too many things I found dislikeable about this novel. Frankly, I skimmed through most of it.<br /><br />Who knew a marriage of convenience story could be so boring?</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11701973595334053932">This was my sixth Heyer, and it is simply awful. After 75 agonizing pages, had to put in the "did-not-finish" shelf.<br /><br />Who knew Heyer could be so bad?<br /><br />OK, on to some of the main problems with this book:<br /><br />Well, the heroine stuttered. Hey, I've got nothing against stuttering (I did it for a while a long time ago), b-but I d-don't w-want to r-read it in a b-book e-every s-sentence the h-heroine m-makes. V-very a-annoying. <br /><br />Heroine is a stupid, vapid one. Perhaps she changes later, but I'm not willing to w</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11701973595334053932">This was my sixth Heyer, and it is simply awful. After 75 agonizing pages, had to put in the "did-not-finish" shelf.<br /><br />Who knew Heyer could be so bad?<br /><br />OK, on to some of the main problems with this book:<br /><br />Well, the heroine stuttered. Hey, I've got nothing against stuttering (I did it for a while a long time ago), b-but I d-don't w-want to r-read it in a b-book e-every s-sentence the h-heroine m-makes. V-very a-annoying. <br /><br />Heroine is a stupid, vapid one. Perhaps she changes later, but I'm not willing to waste more time to find out. I like Heyer heroines when they are mature and charming or when they are frisky and coltish. Not when they are vapid - like Horatia (aka Horry) is. <br /><br />I don't much care for the hero. He's not very charming or dashing or witty like a Heyer hero can be. He's just "there".<br /><br />Definitely <em>not</em> recommended for a first try of Heyer. She's much better then this.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1290673827">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1290673827" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3639005-candi" class="left imgcol" title="Candi"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409332672p2/3639005.jpg" alt="Candi" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1290673827?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 30, 2015</a>

I just love his voice. He really adapted to the different characters so well and I found myself smiling throughout.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText891493408627746692">It took me quite some time to get through this audio book - but not because it wasn't delightful! I cannot seem to find the time to listen to books as well as I can find the time to actually sit and read them. But, this audio version narrated by Richard Armitage was very entertaining. I just love his voice. He really adapted to the different characters so well and I found myself smiling throughout. The story itself was quite good and had a few fun twists that kept things interesting. I enjoyed my first Georgette Heyer novel and would read another. Any narration completed by Richard Armitage would be a bonus no matter what the title!</span>

        rated it

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">regency romance lovers</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer107574336">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8823029799070230937">This was even more delightful the second time around, although it's been so long since my first read that much of this seemed new. It's a light-hearted Georgian romp about a very immature young lady who convinces a very rich Earl that he should offer for her instead of for her beautiful older sister; she's actually doing this out of love of her sister, who loves an army captain but was going to Sacrifice herself in a Marriage of Convenience to get the family out of the mess their brother created</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8823029799070230937">This was even more delightful the second time around, although it's been so long since my first read that much of this seemed new. It's a light-hearted Georgian romp about a very immature young lady who convinces a very rich Earl that he should offer for her instead of for her beautiful older sister; she's actually doing this out of love of her sister, who loves an army captain but was going to Sacrifice herself in a Marriage of Convenience to get the family out of the mess their brother created with his gambling.<br /><br />Although there's an abduction (foiled by the heroine), some more nasty plotting, highway robbers, and a sword fight, this is really a comedy, with some Keystone Kops elements when the heroine's brother and his best friend try to resolve a dilemma that the silly twit (aka heroine) has gotten herself into.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5684956070817193566"><br /><br />Lord Rule reminds me a little of Justin Alastair in These Old Shade, who is my favourite Heyer hero.<br /><br />I loved the scene where Horry, our heroine, goes to visit Lord Rule…<br /><br />‘You m-may have n-noticed that I have a – a stammer.’<br />‘Yes, I had noticed,’ the Earl answered gently.<br />‘If you f-feel you c-can’t bear it, sir, I shall quite understand,’ <br />Horatia said in a small, anxious voice.<br />‘I like it,’ said the Earl.<br /><br />Awww…*sigh*<br /><br /><br />The beginning and the end were the only times I felt any real liking for Horry. The rest of the time she was irritating.<br /><br />Beautifully written, charming and witty, with great minor characters.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1104977095">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1104977095" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/18396754-kathy" class="left imgcol" title="Kathy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1382307134p2/18396754.jpg" alt="Kathy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1104977095?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 12, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18396754-kathy" class="user" title="Kathy">Kathy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/32106.The_Convenient_Marriage" title="Paperback 9780099474425" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1104977095">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18425032690711598883">There are so many things I loved about The Convenient Marriage by Georgette Heyer, but there <br />are a few little hiccoughs that held this one back from being a sparkling gem like, say, Venetia. (Love, love, love Venetia!!). <br /><br />I'm not sure when I enjoyed reading a first meeting between two principals, Horatia Winwood and Marcus Drelincourt, Earl of Rule, more. Horatia 'Horry' may be very young, just 17, but I loved her boldness, her fearlessness, her honesty, and her determination. In one fell swoop,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18425032690711598883">There are so many things I loved about The Convenient Marriage by Georgette Heyer, but there <br />are a few little hiccoughs that held this one back from being a sparkling gem like, say, Venetia. (Love, love, love Venetia!!). <br /><br />I'm not sure when I enjoyed reading a first meeting between two principals, Horatia Winwood and Marcus Drelincourt, Earl of Rule, more. Horatia 'Horry' may be very young, just 17, but I loved her boldness, her fearlessness, her honesty, and her determination. In one fell swoop, Horry frees her older sister, Lizzie, from an unwanted match with the Earl of Rule, ensures Lizzie will marry her true love, Edward Heron, and replenishes the depleted Winwood fortunes. How does a young lady of little beauty, no fortune, a regrettable stammer, and Kahlo-esque eyebrows persuade a man-of-the-world like Marcus Drelincourt, Earl of Rule, to marry her? Well, she charms the pantaloons off him for starters. Of course employing a surprising frontal attack to peaking his interest also didn't hurt her case either.<br /><br />'I'm Horry Winwood.'<br /><br />'Horry', he repeated.<br /><br />'Horatia,' she explained. 'It is an odious name, isn't it? I was given it on account of Mr. Walpole. He is my godfather, you understand.'<br /><br />'Perfectly,' bowed his lordship.'you must forgive me for being so dull-witted, but would you believe it? - I am still quite in the dark.' (pp. 20-21)<br /><br />Although Rule has no idea why Horry has come to his home at this point, this first meeting sets the stage for the rest of their exchanges throughout The Convenient Marriage. I particularly enjoyed Rule's patience and tenderness and humor as he tries to keep up with Horry and her shenanigans, and their first conversation delightfully highlights all of these elements. I loved her too honest explanation of how Charlotte, the middle Winwood sister, 'shrinks from the idea of m-making such a sacrifice, even for Lizzie's sake' as well as Rule's suppressed (but just barely) laughter. Horry, you see, is to be 'the Sacrifice.' <br /><br />'Have I said s-something I shouldn't?' inquired Horatia doubtfully.<br /><br />'On the contrary,' he replied. 'Your conversation is most salutary, Miss Winwood.'<br /><br />'You are laughing at me,' said Horatia accusingly. I d-daresay you think I am very stupid, sir, but indeed, it is most serious.'<br /><br />'I think you are delightful,' said Rule.(p. 22)<br /><br />Horry then spills the beans on how Pelham, her brother, has a fondness for gambling, that Lizzie the Beauty is in love with Edward, and that Charlotte has vowed never to marry. She very forthrightly makes no claim to beauty like Lizzie (though she does have 'the Nose') and lists all her shortcomings:<br /><br />Horatia seemed determined to make a clean breast of her blemishes. 'A-and perhaps you could become used to my eyebrows?'<br /><br />The smile lurked at the back of Rule's eyes. 'I think, quite easily.'<br /><br />She said sadly, 'They won't arch, you know. And I ought to t-tell you that we have quite given up hope of my growing any taller.'<br /><br />'It would remain a pity if you did,' said his lordship.<br /><br />'D-do you think so?' Horatia was surprised. 'It is a great trial for me, I can assure you.' She took a deep breath, and added, with difficulty: 'You m-may have n-noticed that I have a - a stammer.'<br /><br />'Yes, I had noticed,' the Earl answered gently.<br /><br />'If you f-feel you c-can't bear it, sir, I shall quite understand,' Horatia said in a small, anxious voice.<br /><br />'I like it,' said the Earl. (p. 24)<br /><br />It's the 18-year age difference, however, that makes Rule reluctant to go forward with Horry's plan. But Horry assures him the difference is not an issue for her, that she, in fact, wants to marry him. Further, she promises not to 'interfere' in his life, i.e., his mistress. I think the age difference didn't bother me so much because Rule recognizes she is just too young, she isn't ready to settle down. His gentle but firm manner allows her to play at being an adult, to sow a few wild oats as he waits and watches over Horry till she matures. His patience and understanding with Horry's immaturity was one the reasons I fell in love with Rule.<br /><br />After they marry, Horry gets quite a name for herself with her madcap exploits, not the least of which is an unfortunate gambling habit coupled with the Winwood bad luck, racing gigs to St. James on a dare, spending way too much money on all the latest fashions, and befriending Lord Lethridge, not only 'the wildest rake in town' but also a man Rule is rumored to have fought a duel with over Louisa, Rule's sister, years ago. Though Rule doesn't stifle Horry, he's always there quietly and firmly setting things to rights as she entangles herself in scandal after scandal. Even Louisa, who genuinely likes Horry, is exasperated with her antics, concerned that he not only appears foolish but that she'll ruin him socially and financially. When she admonishes him to 'take a stick and beat her', he languidly objects because it would just be too 'fatiguing.' Horry was like a kid in a toy store with an American Express 'black card' for such a long time that I confess I lost patience with her, especially when she flounces. Yes, she's young, but heaven save me from a flouncer!<br /><br />There are a a few times when I noticed Horry is perhaps not particularly proud of her actions. When Lizzie marries her Edward, Horry is aware that Lizzie has probably heard about some of her exploits and is reluctant to engage in 'intimate conversation' with her sister. This seems to show she is embarrassed and maybe a little ashamed. But I also think some of Horry's reluctance for a heart-to-heart with Lizzie is based on a little envy of her sister's love match, a marriage she's just beginning to want with Rule. I also wondered if some of her outrageous behavior was just a way to lure Rule's attention away from Lady Massey, his mistress. Her burgeoning affection for Rule is both confusing and confining to her. After all, she promised not to interfere in his lifestyle, but I think by this time she's yearning for the kind of marriage Lizzie and Edward will have.<br /><br />When and how Horatia falls in love with Rule was a bit muddied and pretty much all 'off screen.' In fact, I was sure she was more interested in Lethridge than Rule for a long time. But there was this surprising glimpse into Horry's thoughts when people began to couple her name with Lethridge's.<br /><br />Attracted to Lethridge she might be, but there was a very cogent reason why she should not be in the least in love with him. The reason stood well over six foot in height, and was going to be shown, in vulgar parlance, that what was sauce for the goose could be sauce for the gander as well.' (p. 149)<br /><br />The sauce being, of course, Lady Massey, and whether Rule is in love with his mistress. So I had to go back and re-read the part where Horry learns just exactly who Lady Massey is. She had had no idea that Lady Massey, whom she's acquainted with, is also Rule's mistress. That bit of information was spitefully hinted at by Crosby Drelincourt, Rule's cousin and heir presumptive and a ridiculous 'Macaroni', as payback for Horry's mockery of his foppishness one night at the opera and was his way to ensure Rule and Horatia's marriage remains 'convenient' and not a love match. Then Horatia sees Rule at Lady Massey's box during intermission, questions her brother, Pelham, and receives confirmation of that lady's status. <br /><br />The candid green eyes lifted to his face. 'Is R-Rule in love with Lady M-Massey?' Horatia asked bluntly.<br /><br />'Oh, nothing like that!' the Viscount assured her. 'These little affairs don't mean being in love, y'know. Burn it, Horry, Rule's a man of the world! There's nothin in it, my dear gal - everyone has 'em.' <br /><br />Horatia glanced across at Lady Massey's box again, but the Earl had disappeared. She swallowed before replying: ' I kn-know. P-please don't think that I m-mind, because I d-don't. Only I think m-might have been told.'<br /><br />'Well, to tell you the truth, I thought you must know,' said Pelham. 'It's common knowledge, and it ain't as though you married Rule for love, after all.'<br /><br />'N-no,' agreed Horatia, rather forlornly. (pp. 111-112)<br /><br />She may not have married Rule for love but it's clear that by this time Horatia's emotions are engaged. Why else would she be 'forlorn' when confronted with evidence of the thing she swore not to interfere with? So perhaps 'muddied' isn't the best word to describe Horatia's journey to falling in love. Perhaps 'subtle' is a much better descriptor. More importantly, I realized this book is much more weighted on Rule's journey than Horry's. He is the star of this show.<br /><br />One of the first 'aha!' scenes in which Rule's nascent feelings for Horry (as well as Horatia's for Rule, I might add) are shown beautifully was in a conversation that ostensibly deals with Horatia's outrageous behavior but also has these tantalizing threads of his yearning for a closer relationship with her. Horatia, returning from a visit to her mother, is petulantly defiant with Rule's request for a moment of her time after she discovers Louisa has been discussing her latest exploits with Rule and suggested that he beat her with a stick.<br /><br />Across the wide stretch of hall the Earl's eyes met and held hers. 'Horry,' he said pleasantly, 'you know how much I dislike exertion. Don't put me to the trouble of fetching you.'<br /><br />The chin came down a little, and the smouldering eyes showed a certain speculative interest. 'C-carry me, do you m-mean? I wonder if you would?'<br /><br />The gravity in Rule's expression was dispelled by slight look of amusement. 'And I wonder whether you really think that I would not?' He said. (p. 79)<br /><br />Horatia is more upset that Rule offered no defense of her to Louisa than talking behind her back or the threat of a beating. Rule explains that Horry makes it hard to defend her when she seems hell bent on shocking the Ton with one outrageous escapade after another. Horatia has been very busy indeed including loosing 'a wild animal on Polite Circles', allowing said 'wild animal', aka a monkey, to visit Rule's breakfast table before he himself enjoys breakfast, not to mention an appallingly thick sheaf of unpaid bills Horry has racked up. One bill in particular has him puzzled and a bit worried:<br /><br />'But - forgive my curiosity, Horry - do you invariably pay a hundred and twenty guineas for a pair of shoes?'<br /><br />'What?' shrieked Horatia. The Earl showed her the bill. She stared at it with dawning consternation. 'Oh!' she said. 'I-I remember now. You s-see, Marcus, they - they have heels studded with emeralds.'<br /><br />'Then the matter becomes comprehensible.' (p. 81)<br /><br />You just have to love a man who understands paying mega bucks for shoes. Though he has couched this discussion in terms of questioning Horry's extravagances as well as curbing her desire to shock the Ton, it is his next statement that points to a more relevant reason for this 'Come to Jesus' meeting. <br /><br />'I wore them at the Subscription Ball at Almack's. They are called venez-y-voir, you know.'<br /><br />'That would account, no doubt,' remarked Rule, 'for the presence of the three young gentlemen whom I found - er - assisting you at your toilet that evening.'<br /><br />'B-but there is nothing in that, Rule!' objected Horatia, lifting her downcast head. ' it is quite the thing for gentlemen to be admitted as soon as the under-dress is on. I know it is, b-because Lady Stokes d-does it. They advise one how to p-place one's p-patches, and where to bestow one's flowers, and what p-perfume to use.'<br /><br />If the Earl of Rule found anything amusing in being instructed by his bride in the art of dalliance the only sign he gave of it was the very faintest quiver of the lips. 'Ah!' he said. 'And yet -' he looked down at her, half-smiling - 'And yet I believe I might advise you in these matters to even better purpose.'<br /><br />'B-but you're my husband,' Horatia pointed out.<br /><br />He turned back to the bills. 'That is undoubtedly a handicap,' he admitted. (pp. 81-82)<br /><br />He laughs with her about the monkey stealing Crosby's wig, lightly dismisses her allowing the monkey to dine at his breakfast table, and commiserates on the exorbitant price of being fashionable, but he stumbles a bit on the words 'found -er assisting' her toilet. So I asked myself: Why was he visiting her boudoir while she's dressing, hmmmm? A reconnaissance mission maybe on the state of Horatia's emotions? At the very least it signifies more than just curiosity about his wife. Plus, he actively seeks her out not only this time but others as well. Though he still appears amused by Horry's behavior, I believe there was a little disappointment that she turned to those 'young' men for assistance with patches, etc. There's also the fact that he has begun to pull away from Caroline Massey. The lady herself notes as much soon after this scene.<br /><br />Rule's hides his disappointment of his wife's allowing 'young' gentleman into her boudoir behind a languidly ironic admission that being a husband is indeed a disadvantage, but this is not the only clue that he sees, or is beginning to see, Horatia as more than a 'convenient' wife. Rule is completely imperturbable as far as Horatia's shocking behavior, showing no more than a consistently placid humorous acceptance of all of her peccadilloes. Except for one thing. He is indeed worried, very worried, about her 'excessive gaming.' Her inability to pay her bills from his very generous provisions troubles him enough that instead of a languid smile and an airy dismissal, he ...frowns. I wasn't sure Rule knew how to frown till now. <br /><br />He laid the bills down. 'Pharaoh, Horry?'<br /><br />'Oh n-no, not all of it!' she said eagerly, glad to be able to produce an extenuating circumstance. 'B-basset!'<br /><br />'I see.'<br /><br />The note of amusement had left his voice; she ventured to raise her eyes, and saw something very like a frown on his face. (pp. 82-83)<br /><br />He's not even aware of the frown till Horry points it out, and he quickly reverts to the 'frippery fellow' who finds most things in life, er, fatiguing. If this were a game of poker, Rule just gave away his hand. Unfortunately, Horatia doesn't recognize it for what it is.<br /><br />Rule is such wonderful character. To misquote Ogden Nash, Rule would have been content to live his life 'in nonchalance and insouciance were it not for' loving Horatia 'which is rather a nouciance' for him, I'm sure. He is a steel trap wrapped in blue velvet, with a fall of lace ruffles and a perruque a bourse who languidly pinches snuff, eschews an involvement in 'Politicks' and 'Debates in the House' for invitations to card parties written on pink paper, and enjoys making life for his 'prince of a secretary' Mr. Gisborne the veriest trial. Yet the man who just can't be arsed engages in a sword fight for his wife's honor with Lethridge that rivals the one between Inigo Montoya and Westley. Rule who upon learning of his cousin's involvement in coupling Lethridge's name with Horatia's tells Crosby he's lucky Pelham fought a duel with him because he would have used a cane on him instead of a small word and cuts Crosby off financially. How energized Rule becomes where Horatia is concerned! Not to mention the Mysterious Man in the Scarlet Domino. What a great scene that was! <br /><br />For my tastes, I didn't care for Lady Massey's continued presence, though admittedly tangential and infrequent, after Horry and Rule's marriage. There are only a few exchanges between the two - one before the marriage (the most bothersome for me) but after Rule's engagement, Rule's infamous visit to Lady Massey's opera box after their marriage, and a classic misunderstanding scene Horry witnesses at Vaux Hall. While I didn't like the way Lady Massey was painted broadly as the stereotypical manipulative other woman, I found Rule's willingness to continue seeing her after he marries Horry despite knowing that she is not and never will be faithful to him a little repulsive. He tells Caroline that he didn't ask her to marry him because marriage with him would be dull and:<br /><br />'You see, my dear, to use your own words, you would have to love me - only me.' (p. 58)<br /><br />I suppose it's in character for him (at that particular time) to just continue seeing Caroline Massey since he would probably consider it too 'fatiguing' to find another mistress while he waited for Horatia to grow up. But that smacks of shallowness of character as well as a healthy dose of Ye Olde Double Standard crap to me. I was encouraged, however, to see that the more Horatia becomes a part of his life and his heart, the more he pulls away from Caroline Massey. <br /><br />Additionally, the ending was a little abrupt. I really wish some of the latter hi-jinks with Pelham, his friend, Pom, and Captain Heron had been shortened in favor of a little longer ending between Horry and Rule. Although I will admit I laughed out loud at the scene of these three and Hawkins the Highwayman teaming up to waylaying unsuspecting travelers along the road to London. <br /><br />I loved the light and playful tone as well as the humor sprinkled throughout The Convenient Marriage. When Rule is on stage, he really is the star, a very strong charismatic personality, and one I enjoyed reading a lot. Though Horatia's wore on my nerves a bit (just say no to flouncing!), I loved her imperfection. Lethridge as the villain is very well realized and almost as fascinating as Marcus Drelincourt, the Earl of Rule is. That's one book I wish had been written. Despite a few problems, The Convenient Marriage is very much worth spending a few hours with as well as earning a place on my keeper shelf.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18425032690711598883">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_50741280">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50741280" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/827478-nicole-prestin" class="left imgcol" title="Nicole Prestin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1400718302p2/827478.jpg" alt="Nicole Prestin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/50741280?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 28, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/827478-nicole-prestin" class="user" title="Nicole Prestin">Nicole Prestin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/32106.The_Convenient_Marriage" title="Paperback 9780099474425" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/827478-nicole-prestin?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer50741280">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17751214384197323505">This was one of those books that totally surprised me. I had no idea that Georgette Heyer was considered one of the founders of the historical romance genre. I also had no idea that one of my favorite Sci-Fi authors, Lois McMaster Bujold, was a fan and influenced by these books (although now that I've read a few of Heyer's books, it's really, really obvious). I was just looking for a good trashy romance read and picked this up simply because of the title. But as I read, I realized that this was</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17751214384197323505">This was one of those books that totally surprised me. I had no idea that Georgette Heyer was considered one of the founders of the historical romance genre. I also had no idea that one of my favorite Sci-Fi authors, Lois McMaster Bujold, was a fan and influenced by these books (although now that I've read a few of Heyer's books, it's really, really obvious). I was just looking for a good trashy romance read and picked this up simply because of the title. But as I read, I realized that this was far superior to 99% of romance novels I've ever read.<br /><br />The male lead, Lord Rule, is pretty cool. But then, his archetype of lazy fop, who's really rather badass and smart underneath it all, is one of my favorites so I admit I'm biased there.<br /><br />But the characters who totally steal the show about halfway through the book, and who I wish had a novel just about their goofy antics, are Horatia's brother and his best friend. The scenes between them are absolutely HILARIOUS (especially the scene where they... uh... "rescue" Horatia), and there were several times when I found myself actually laughing out loud at what was going on.<br /><br />The one drawback of the novel is that the heroine Horatia is somewhat immature, idiotic and annoying and I could never quite understand why someone like Lord Rule would fall in love with her. However, she's still moderately entertaining because even though she's a dumbass, people in the book realize this and treat her accordingly. Plus, she's a compulsive gambler, which is something I've never, ever seen in a romance heroine before and makes her kind of interesting. <br /><br />Anyway, this book is a tremendous amount of fun. I wouldn't necessarily recommend it to people who don't like period romances, but if you enjoy romance with a healthy dose of screwball comedy this might be for you.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17751214384197323505">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer588189868">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7424439851881649395"><strong>Update on 06/21/14</strong>: Just finished listening to the audiobook. How on earth did I only give this 4 stars the first time?!? This book is swoony (the fact that the audiobook was read by Richard Armitage <em>may</em> have something to do with it...). Proper review coming :D<br /><br />Another good read from Heyer!!! I abseloutely loved the characters!!! Horatia wasn't my favourite heroine ever, but I still liked her. I just found her slighty too frivolous and immature for Rule; she would have needed to grow and mature m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7424439851881649395"><strong>Update on 06/21/14</strong>: Just finished listening to the audiobook. How on earth did I only give this 4 stars the first time?!? This book is swoony (the fact that the audiobook was read by Richard Armitage <em>may</em> have something to do with it...). Proper review coming :D<br /><br />Another good read from Heyer!!! I abseloutely loved the characters!!! Horatia wasn't my favourite heroine ever, but I still liked her. I just found her slighty too frivolous and immature for Rule; she would have needed to grow and mature more than she actually did, for them to be a good match. I loved Rule, especially in the second half of the book when he finally starts to do things and everyone thinks he doesn't know anything but he does and I thought he was pretty good at manipulating people... But I must admit that I had a penchant for Lethbridge...I don't know why, he just sounded so...hot. Heyer made him sound really hot. And badass, of course. I thought he was one of the best "villains" ever. He reminded me a lot of the Duke of Andover in "The Black Moth". I really like those cynical, dark, mysterious, sarcastic and good-looking villains. They make the story ten times more fun and interesting. I also have to give much credit to Pom and Pel for making this story so hilarious!!! Without them it wouldn't have been half as good! Their habit of betting on every thing was just too funny!!<br />Overall, I thought "The Convenient Marriage" was a very pleasant read, definitely recommended for Heyer fans. Horatia's stutter may become annoying, but it's part of her and of her personality, and just keep reminding yourself that Rule thought it was "charming"!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7424439851881649395">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_180631406">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180631406" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1179112-katie" class="left imgcol" title="Katie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1264370303p2/1179112.jpg" alt="Katie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/180631406?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 29, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1179112-katie" class="user" title="Katie">Katie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/32106.The_Convenient_Marriage" title="Paperback 9780099474425" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer180631406">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2260595634093011388">This book starts out with a hilariously honest conversation between a suitor and the younger sister of the girl he wants to marry. She argues that if all he wants is a wife, he should marry her because her sister is IN LOVE with someone else. Strangely he agrees.<br /><br />The great thing about this book is that Horatia is actually, genuinely normal. She stutters and is described as the least attractive of her sisters. And yet! She's the heroine! It's not an ugly duckling story because she stutters and is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2260595634093011388">This book starts out with a hilariously honest conversation between a suitor and the younger sister of the girl he wants to marry. She argues that if all he wants is a wife, he should marry her because her sister is IN LOVE with someone else. Strangely he agrees.<br /><br />The great thing about this book is that Horatia is actually, genuinely normal. She stutters and is described as the least attractive of her sisters. And yet! She's the heroine! It's not an ugly duckling story because she stutters and is normal looking for the whole book.<br /><br />Then in usual Heyer fashion it all goes hilariously hijinxy. It turns out that much like her brother, she's got a weakness for gambling. At first this seems like a flaw, but then it becomes awesome because her brother likes to bet on EVERYTHING.<br /><br />When Horatia is running away from a man who kidnapped her and who she thinks she killed she runs into her brother and his friend and the conversation goes like this:<br /><br />Horatia: Oh, good, it's my brother, Pelham! Pelham! I think I killed this lord!<br />Pelham: I bet you didn't. Bet you 2 to 1 that she didn't kill him Pom.<br />Pom: I'll take that. 2 to 1, you say?<br /><br />I think one of my favorite things about this book is that there is no Big Misunderstandings. The hero never thinks ill of his wife, and explicitly says at one point that he's "no Othello". Which, props to Heyer for that.<br /><br />Plus, the whole tone of the book is so delightful.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2260595634093011388">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_99323996">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99323996" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/237469-miriam" class="left imgcol" title="Miriam"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1275489063p2/237469.jpg" alt="Miriam" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/99323996?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 21, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/237469-miriam" class="user" title="Miriam">Miriam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/32106.The_Convenient_Marriage" title="Paperback 9780099474425" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer99323996">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3769065587455029925">Seventeen-year-old Horatia marries Rule to save her family from penury after her brother Pelham gambles away their fortune. Rule originally intended to marry the beautiful older sister Elizabeth, but she is in love with an army officer named Edward. Rule is intrigued by Horry's forthright speech and unpolished manners. Unfortunately, he doesn't take the initiative and tell her he likes her, instead letting her believe it is merely a marriage of convenience. Since she is not beautiful and knows a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3769065587455029925">Seventeen-year-old Horatia marries Rule to save her family from penury after her brother Pelham gambles away their fortune. Rule originally intended to marry the beautiful older sister Elizabeth, but she is in love with an army officer named Edward. Rule is intrigued by Horry's forthright speech and unpolished manners. Unfortunately, he doesn't take the initiative and tell her he likes her, instead letting her believe it is merely a marriage of convenience. Since she is not beautiful and knows about Rule's mistress, Horry reasonably enough assumes he doesn't care much about her. But because she really does love him, she can't resist the urge to try and make him jealous by flirting with Lethbridge, whom she does not realize is an old rival of her husband, using her to get back at Rule.<br /><br />I found both characters likable but was impatient with her lack of sense and his laziness. This story seems like a model for those dumb "stupid misunderstanding" romances that abound. On the other hand, there may be some intended social commentary on the emotional unhealthiness of a society in which behavior is so artificial that it is more acceptable to discuss one's affairs than one's feelings for a spouse.<br /><br />One thing I liked was Heyer's rejection of the creepy "forced seduction" or "I rape you because I love you" theme that has hung around in romance for way too long. When Lethbridge tries this on Horry it is obvious that neither loves the other at all:<br /><br />In this scene Lethbridge has just kidnapped Horry and tells her he is going to ravish her.<br /><em>"I wish," said Horatia forcefully, "you would stop talking about love. It makes me feel sick."<br />"My dear," he said, "I assure you I am falling deeper in love with you every moment."<br />She curled her lip. "Stuff!" she snorted. "If you loved me the least little bit, you wouldn't do this to me."</em><br /><br />This is really the normal, well-adjusted reaction to a scene of this nature. Rape is not romantic. This book demonstrates that creating weird psychological tests for your partner and withholding your feelings are not very romantic, either. I enjoyed reading it, especially the farcical elements, but couldn't help thinking that the whole story could have been circumvented by Horry and Rule having one honest conversation on their honeymoon.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3769065587455029925">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_833682127">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/833682127" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11349024-becca" class="left imgcol" title="Becca"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423870283p2/11349024.jpg" alt="Becca" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/833682127?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 22, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11349024-becca" class="user" title="Becca">Becca</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6058948-the-convenient-marriage" title="Paperback 9781402217722" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11349024-becca?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer833682127">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9592551112348387765"><em>The Convenient Marriage</em> was the second novel of Georgette Heyer’s that I encountered, by way of an audio-book, and I can easily say it was just as pleasant as the first, <em>Venetia</em>. The audio-book that I listened to was an abridged version, narrated by Richard Armitage. I do so hate it when audio-books are abridged; generally because I dislike missing out on any part of the story, and more particularly in this case because anything that kept Richard Armitage talking for longer had to be a good thin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9592551112348387765"><em>The Convenient Marriage</em> was the second novel of Georgette Heyer’s that I encountered, by way of an audio-book, and I can easily say it was just as pleasant as the first, <em>Venetia</em>. The audio-book that I listened to was an abridged version, narrated by Richard Armitage. I do so hate it when audio-books are abridged; generally because I dislike missing out on any part of the story, and more particularly in this case because anything that kept Richard Armitage talking for longer had to be a good thing. <br /><br />Though I mostly prefer to read for myself, I have listened to my fair share of audio-books in my time, and I can say without exaggeration that Richard Armitage is the most pleasant and talented reader I’ve ever heard. He reads at a tolerable speed, not maddeningly slow (like some), and his voice is very nice to listen to in the narrative sequences. Furthermore, as far as one person can do all the voices in a story – not that I ever found that a brilliant idea in the first place - he does a wonderful job of all of them, from Lord Rule’s languorous, faintly amused tone to Lady Massey’s soft and scheming voice. Most amusing I found was Crosby’s rather highly pitched, quavering, condescending whine, though I hate to think what that would have done to Richard’s voice after a while. And really, his depiction of Horatia’s stammer is the most adorable thing I have ever heard.<br /><br />Quiet apart from the brilliance of the narration, the plot of <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> left almost nothing to be desired, at least for me. Now having read (or listened to) quite a few of Georgette Heyers novels, I must say - at the risk of ruffling many sensibilities – that I enjoy her work just a little better than Jane Austen's. Not consistently, mind you, but some of Georgette’s work I do find to be more enjoyable than Jane’s. But I’ll gloss over that before I go down in a hail of rotten fruit and various other unpleasant missiles. I freely admit to some disappointment upon finding out from the blurb that the marriage of the story occurred right at the start of the novel. I thought, rather foolishly as it turns out; <em>”Where can the romance be in a story where the two people involved are married within a couple of chapters?”</em> I don’t think I’ve ever had a more wrong thought in my life.<br /><br />The two people involved in the romance are Horatia Winwood, youngest daughter of Lady Winwood, and the thirty-five year old Earl of Rule. The Earl – Marcus Drellincourt - has offered for Horatia’s older sister, Elizabeth, who feels she must accept the offer to gain the Earl’s fortune and save the family from poverty due to their brother’s gambling debts. But in order to do this, Elizabeth will have to give up all hope of marrying her true love, the respectable but poor Edward Heron. Horatia, the youngest of the Winwood sisters, who is a sweet and incorrigible young thing, very bravely decides to take matters into her own hands. So Horatia - who, stammering and rather plain at first seems to have little to recommend her - visits the Earl of Rule, and boldly asks if he will marry her in place of her beautiful sister Elizabeth.<br /><br />If there is one chapter of <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> I could call my favourite, (though there are many chapters I love) it would be the second chapter, in which Horatia and the Earl of Rule meet for the first time. When meeting the Earl, Horatia is sweet, shy and stammering, while at the same time irrepressible, impulsive, and determined. The Earl of Rule was faultlessly polite, unfailingly kind and gentle in dealing with her unconventional request, and generally adept at hiding his amusement. I don’t want to give too much away about the scene, but I will say that almost every moment warmed my heart just a little more, and by the time the Earl of Rule had agreed to marry Horatia (and no, that isn’t a spoiler, just look at the title…), I had decided that this scene was the sweetest thing I had ever read. Okay, strictly speaking I heard it, but let’s not split hairs, because if anything Armitage’s depiction of the characters only added to the delightfulness of the scene. So yes, not to linger too long on the point, soon enough Horatia marries Marcus Drellincourt and becomes the Countess of Rule. <br /><br />However, very soon into the marriage it became clear that Horatia, disappointingly enough, was getting all too carried away with being so very rich so suddenly. Though perhaps this behaviour is understandable in a newly married young woman at the tender age of seventeen, I am the same age, and I declare I should be far too embarrassed to squander the fortune of a man I had only just married. It would feel far too much like taking advantage. Though as both Horatia and Lord Rule agree, “One must dress…”, and I cannot blame any young woman for wanting to buy pretty and fashionable clothing. No, it was only the gambling that I objected to in Horatia’s behaviour, especially as she had already seen its decidedly negative effects on her brother Pelham. I mean, for goodness sake, their whole family was in debt because of gambling! Surely that would be enough to dissuade one from such a pursuit. <br /><br />Aside from her “fatal tendency to gamble”, I found Horatia to be an exceedingly lovely heroine. I’ve no doubt she has been condemned by others as hopelessly naïve, and a little clueless, but there is something refreshing in a heroine so completely devoid of any true malice, and one refusing to believe the worst about people. Unfortunately she has to learn, throughout the course of the story, that sometimes people believe the worst about others with good reason. Despite her imperfections which can be ascribed to the inexperience of a teenager, Horry has a good heart, a fierce loyalty to those that she loves, and a sort of innocence about her that is endlessly endearing.<br /><br />Lord Rule was of an altogether different character to that of his young wife, and there were times near the beginning of the novel where I wondered why I even liked him. His affair with Lady Massey was something I could not but disapprove of, as well as the fact that he did seem to be a little less than energetic in his pursuits. Perhaps I would have minded this more had I not been so delighted by his intellectual exploits. His deceptive mildness was charming to read about, I often found myself smothering a smile at his very cleverly constructed remarks, or the way people constantly underestimated his cunning and intelligence. Furthermore, his nature was kind, and the patient way in which he dealt with his turbulent young wife demanded respect, endearing him to me greatly. Horatia and Rule were an odd mix, I’ll admit, but together there was something about the two of them that simply made my heart melt. The last point in Rule’s favour was this; when a character appears to be so unconcerned about everything, it is all the more wonderful when he shows strong feelings, or takes matters into his own hands. <br /><br />Of course, the character that made such an effort necessary was Robert, Baron Lethbridge. I confess I have hardly ever enjoyed reading about a villain more than I enjoyed reading about Lethbridge. His manners were intriguing, his intelligence clear, and he seemed to bear many of the hallmarks of a Regency hero. In fact, in the beginning, if you didn’t know he was a villain you… wouldn’t know he was a villain. As odd as that sounds, it makes perfect sense to me. Georgette Heyer makes it clear that Robert <em>is</em> a villain, but her genius is in making you hope, making you almost believe that he is genuine. Certainly you could not blame poor Horatia for being taken in. <br /><br />If the tentative, complicated romance between Horatia and the Earl of Rule made up one half of my enjoyment of the novel, certainly the schemes and counter-schemes between Robert Lethbridge and Marcus Drellincourt made up the other half of my enjoyment. While someone with a more actively discerning eye might have guessed at some of the schemes, there were instances where I found myself either shocked, pleasantly surprised, or helplessly giggling. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[One scheme, however, was completely and utterly transparent. I mean, even I could see through that whole highwaymen thing. Who in their right mind would believe three highwaymen would simply leave upon being spotted by a lone rider? That would only happen in the most cliché of romances, and if it did happen, it would certainly be the husband and not the rival who would ride in and save the day. I admit to hoping, just for the merest instant, that it was the Earl of Rule who had ridden up to save Horatia, but as soon as Lethbridge was mentioned… poof went that scheme. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Now I have only one more thing to say on the matter of Robert Lethbridge, and I hope any readers will forgive me for having to go on this massive rant. Which, incidentally, is also a huge spoiler, so please don’t read it unless you’ve read the book. Or unless you really don’t care about spoilers. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[Oh. My. God. That sword-fighting scene. Be still my beating heart! Quite aside from my swooning over Rule’s spirited defence of his wife, and hoping that he wouldn’t be wounded, there are a few lines I simply have to speak about. These lines mark the moment where my anger and strong dislike of Robert Lethbridge on my part boiled over into searing hatred:<br /><br />“I don’t boast of my conquests, Rule,” Lethbridge said softly, and saw the Earl’s hand clench involuntarily. “I shall explain nothing.”<br /><br />Though that line is not in the audio version (I have actually read the paperback also, by the way) these lines are;<br /><br />“Why did my wife enter your house?” He parried too late; the Earl’s point flashed under his guard, checked, and withdrew. He realized that he had been spared, would be spared again, and yet again, until Rule had his answer. He grinned savagely. His words came on his heaving breaths: “Kidnapped—her.” The swords rang together, disengaged. “And then?” He set his teeth; his guard wavered; he recovered it miraculously; the hilt felt slippery in his wet grasp. “And then?” “I do not—boast—of my—conquests!” he panted, and put forth the last remnant of his strength to beat the attack he knew would end the bout.”<br /><br />That final line of dialogue brought involuntary tears to my eyes. While Lethbridge had up until that point proved he was altogether detestable, I had stopped just short of complete and utter hatred. Perhaps a tiny part of me was still looking for something redeemable in him. But to say such a thing, while knowing what torment the words would bring to Rule’s heart… I could not bear the thought. Even worse, Lethbridge would have killed him had he been able – I believe – and left Rule to die thinking that the wife he loved had suffered so terribly in Lethbridge’s hands. Of course, Lethbridge finally admits to Marcus that he committed no such act, but he also admits that had he not been hit on the back of the head with the poker, he would have done so. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Moving on from characters, then; Georgette Heyer’s style of writing is lovely, and the use of her words all but perfect. Every word used in her sentences conveys just the right nuance of meaning. The plot is well paced, the characters wonderful, and while her talent for drama sometimes had me in tears, her shining humour kept me smiling and laughing during those slightly less serious parts of the book. I don’t wish to spoil anything, so I won’t be too detailed… But there was a certain incident involving a fishpond that meant I had to almost run away from my family when I heard it, only just managing to reach my room. At which point I went off into very unladylike giggles and peals of laughter; as Georgette Heyer would say, I was “quite in stitches”.<br /><br />(If anyone’s wondering why I didn’t want my family to hear me laughing, it was because I couldn’t be bothered explaining <em>why</em> I was laughing. They aren’t quite the type of people to understand Georgette Heyer’s vein of humour, and I would have felt ridiculous for laughing so hard only to find them completely indifferent. *sigh*) <br /><br />I do usually talk quite a bit more about the plot of a novel in my reviews. It is only because I love this story so much that I am hesitant to give away a great deal about it, but rest assured the ending left nothing to be desired. Admittedly the chapters beforehand dragged on just a little, with certain people getting into one too many scrapes *cough* Pelham *cough*. But the ending was sweet, and touching… and altogether perfect. I could ask nothing more of <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> than what it had delivered. A light humour, an intriguing plot, loveable characters, detestable yet complicated villains, beautiful writing, and a romance that was quite out of the ordinary. Combine that with Richard Armitage as a narrator, and the delightful snippets of music between chapters, and <em>The Convenient Marriage</em> comes as close to perfect as anyone can ask of a story. <br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9592551112348387765">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/501643119?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11828657-elevetha" class="user" title="Elevetha ">Elevetha </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/7958128-the-convenient-marriage" title="Audio CD 9781843794417" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11828657-elevetha?shelf=regency" class="actionLinkLite">regency</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11828657-elevetha?shelf=audios" class="actionLinkLite">audios</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11828657-elevetha?shelf=2013" class="actionLinkLite">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer501643119">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17895641641207665927">3.5 stars.<br /><br /> I enjoyed the audio version to a degree of about 5 stars. I mean, Richard, honestly....And then the fact that I listened to it with my Awesome Older Sister and we sat in her room, drinking Mountain Dew and Sparkling Apple Cider and eating Hershey's Cookie 'n Cream bars and Nuggets (yum), giggling, and commenting. So much fun....So, yeah. <br /><br /><br />I'll be honest: when I found out that Richard Armitage read this, I couldn't wait to get my hands on a copy of the audio version. <br /><br />His voice is specta</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17895641641207665927">3.5 stars.<br /><br /> I enjoyed the audio version to a degree of about 5 stars. I mean, Richard, honestly....And then the fact that I listened to it with my Awesome Older Sister and we sat in her room, drinking Mountain Dew and Sparkling Apple Cider and eating Hershey's Cookie 'n Cream bars and Nuggets (yum), giggling, and commenting. So much fun....So, yeah. <br /><br /><br />I'll be honest: when I found out that Richard Armitage read this, I couldn't wait to get my hands on a copy of the audio version. <br /><br />His voice is spectacular. Truly. I <s>swooned over</s> adored the narrative bits. <br /><br />Because there are quite a few female characters in this book and because he gives each character their own unique voice, it was almost comical hearing him do female voices. But I loved that as well. <br /><br />......<br /><br />Horatia: Sooo. You're proposing marriage to my older sister? But I'm here to ask you not to marry her because she's in love with somebody else. So, since you want to marry into our family and we're in debt and, really, why not, you can marry me!<br /><br />Earl: You don't have to marry me. I'm perfectly content to agree not to marry your sister....<br /><br />Horatia: No, no. You don't understand. I want to marry you. And I want you to mentor my older sister's man. You'll do that, right? Of course you will. There is no way you're getting out of this that easily. You're marrying me too, right? Yeah, of course you are. <br /><br />Earl: But you're so young...<br /><br />Horatia: Oh, pshaw. I don't mind. And if I don't mind, you shouldn't either, 'kay? You don't mind I have a stutter, do you?<br /><br />Earl: No, no. It's quite charming.<br /><br />Horatia: .....Well, that's a first.<br /><br />....<br /><br />I wasn't sure how I felt about Rule having an evil mistress of doom in the beginning. No, actually, I was sure. I was indignant. And then in the middle of the book, "He's STILL seeing her??!!" <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ But he wasn't. Not really. And anytime he popped out "to see her", he was just accomplishing his goal to make Horry jealous...It was all good then.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
